  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f38d515d-8958-40c6-ad4c-f682ca08fe02'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer14728827544791384383">In the shadow of an abandoned castle, a wolf pack seeks shelter. The she-wolf's pups will not be able to survive the harsh Transylvanian winter. And they are being stalked by a lone wolf, Morgra, possessed of a mysterious and terrifying power known as the Sight. Morgra knows that one of the pups born beneath the castle holds a key to power even stronger than her own power </span>
  <span id="freeText14728827544791384383" style="display:none">In the shadow of an abandoned castle, a wolf pack seeks shelter. The she-wolf's pups will not be able to survive the harsh Transylvanian winter. And they are being stalked by a lone wolf, Morgra, possessed of a mysterious and terrifying power known as the Sight. Morgra knows that one of the pups born beneath the castle holds a key to power even stronger than her own power that could give her control of this world and the next. But the pack she hunts will do anything to protect their own, even if it means setting in motion a battle that will involve all of nature, including the creature the wolves fear the most: Man.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_125320929" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125320929" />
    <a title="Jackie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1660443-jackie"><img alt="Jackie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1225124705p2/1660443.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125320929?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackie" href="/user/show/1660443-jackie">Jackie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1660443-jackie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1660443-jackie?shelf=supernatural">supernatural</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125320929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15583162120410824112">I thought I would love The Sight because it's about wolves but the author knows nothing about wolf behavior and it's annoying me. The things he makes mistakes on are basic behaviors; I feel he was lazy and didn't do his research. He could have found this stuff out in a matter of minutes. <br/>For example, all wolves participate in the rearing of the pups, the author has only the females taking care of the pups. Fact: when pups are born, all members of the pack (male and female) secrete prolactin, 'th</span>
  <span id="freeText15583162120410824112" style="display:none">I thought I would love The Sight because it's about wolves but the author knows nothing about wolf behavior and it's annoying me. The things he makes mistakes on are basic behaviors; I feel he was lazy and didn't do his research. He could have found this stuff out in a matter of minutes. <br />For example, all wolves participate in the rearing of the pups, the author has only the females taking care of the pups. Fact: when pups are born, all members of the pack (male and female) secrete prolactin, 'the nurturing hormone', resulting in all members assisting in the rearing the pups. This is integral to the bonding process that is the single most important aspect of the wolfpack.<br />The author has non-family members in the same pack. This is a rare occurance as packs are formed of blood-ties, family members, again, integral to the bonding process. On the rare occasion when a pack accepts an outsider, that wolf is given the lowly and undesirable positon of Omega. The author has an outsider as the Omega but he makes the mistake of having other members as outsiders and non family members. <br />And the biggest faux paw (you get the pun?) is having a second breeding couple. Never would a couple usurp the Alpha male and female's right to be the sole procreators, for many reasons, the most important being it's not in the best interest of the pack, the pack could not sustain such a large number of pups with 2 breeding couples. A couple must leave and form it's own pack if it wants to breed. The author also makes the mistake of the timing of the births, the non-cooperation of the pack, these things just cannot be if a pack is to survive. <br />Another mistake: an enemy pack walking mere feet away from our pack, and the rival pack doesn't smell them? It's all about the scent for wolves, they don't rely on sight. It's just so inaccurate that I won't bore you with the myriad mistakes I've found because we could be here a long time. <br />When I read a book, even novels, I want the author to do his research. And Clement-Davies is seriously lacking in this area. It's too bad, because it might have been a better book.<br /><br />Besides all the inaccuracies, it's just not that interesting. It could have been, with the legends the author added but he goes about it too slow. I'm halfway through and not much has happened with the legend since the initial hook. I don't rush to get back to it. I can't continue with this book at this point, maybe at another time. <br /><br /><b>Some months later:</b> I eventually went back and finished it and my rating stands for the same reasons as halfway through. The only reason I finished it is I believe I can't say I hated a book with finality until I've finished it. And I think it'll get better (which they never do, sigh). <br />I can definitively say This Book Sucks from start to finish. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15583162120410824112" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17098176483755446444">This was a young-teenage favourite of mine, which I found comfort in returning to whenever the world got too much to handle. I would begin and drop the book at random intervals: sometimes starting the book from the last place I left off, other times at the beginning, but more often from whatever chapter the book fell open at.<br/><br/>I have no idea why exactly it brought me so much solace, as much of the events that occur are startlingly bleak.   <span id="freeText17098176483755446444" style="display:none">This was a young-teenage favourite of mine, which I found comfort in returning to whenever the world got too much to handle. I would begin and drop the book at random intervals: sometimes starting the book from the last place I left off, other times at the beginning, but more often from whatever chapter the book fell open at.<br /><br />I have no idea why exactly it brought me so much solace, as much of the events that occur are startlingly bleak. I can only imagine that it brought me peace to channel my young teenage angst into events so far displaced from reality. That, combined with this being my first introduction to fiction of this type, made this one of my beloved favourites and it will forever hold a nostalgic place in my heart.<br /><br />Returning to it as an adult didn't bring with it exactly the same level of joy but I still appreciated the story and will forever reserve a special place in my heart for this book.</span>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">Avid Readers looking for a heart-wrenching experience</span></div>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jeffrey by: </span>
      A friend reading the sequel (Fell)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/934095-jeffrey?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer16181556&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16181556" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16021286534438887547">It was a stunning book. Stunning. I mean, it hit me and grabbed onto me and sucked me right into it.<br/><br/>I guess I do have to admit, it was a hard read. 500+ pages of very long stuff. If you start scanning the book, you get utterly confused.<br/>And everyone dies. The main character dies. All the good characters die... It's heartbreaking for the most part. Depressing...<br/><br/>But it was extremely vivid, and as soon as you're drawn into the story, it is amazing. The Sight is that type of book that makes your hear</span>
  <span id="freeText16021286534438887547" style="display:none">It was a stunning book. Stunning. I mean, it hit me and grabbed onto me and sucked me right into it.<br /><br />I guess I do have to admit, it was a hard read. 500+ pages of very long stuff. If you start scanning the book, you get utterly confused.<br />And everyone dies. The main character dies. All the good characters die... It's heartbreaking for the most part. Depressing...<br /><br />But it was extremely vivid, and as soon as you're drawn into the story, it is amazing. The Sight is that type of book that makes your heart start beating fast, and you fall in love with the characters.<br /><br />The Sight is a wolf book. The more or less average fantasy "role-playing-plot" (those online forums which players write in words and 'roleplay' as their own fictional characters for a overall story)<br />Basically, in one sentence, the Sight is described as:<br />A family of wolves is torn apart where the main character must stop these evil wolves from enslaving everything, while one by one everyone dies.<br /><br />It's a pretty hard book to get into, and you absolutely have to read very carefully the book. Meaning, if you don't, you still won't be able to tell the difference between a Drappa and Dragga by the end of the book. There's a lot of made up words in the wolf-language, and paying attention to the somewhat boring beginning is everything.<br /><br />Most of all... just read... I hear people I have cried from reading this heartwrenching book. It's very emotional.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16021286534438887547" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_82007870" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82007870" />
    <a title="Leslie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3073380-leslie"><img alt="Leslie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263646983p2/3073380.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82007870?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leslie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leslie" href="/user/show/3073380-leslie">Leslie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who loves fantasy, wolves, ancient folklore, or all of the above!</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82007870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15969398482267599930"><b>Wow.<b> 'Wow' is probably the only word that really describes this book. From its begining to amazing ending, David Clemet-Davies' 'The Sight' is definately one of the best books I've ever read.<br/>In its first chapter, Clement-Davies introduces us to the strange and dangerous world of ancient Transylvania through the eyes of a wolf. Transylvania is both riveting and terrifying, where danger and beauty is everywhere. In a vast forest, we meet Huttser and his mate Palla, two alpha wolves, the head pair </b></b></span>
  <span id="freeText15969398482267599930" style="display:none"><b>Wow.<b> 'Wow' is probably the only word that really describes this book. From its begining to amazing ending, David Clemet-Davies' 'The Sight' is definately one of the best books I've ever read.<br />In its first chapter, Clement-Davies introduces us to the strange and dangerous world of ancient Transylvania through the eyes of a wolf. Transylvania is both riveting and terrifying, where danger and beauty is everywhere. In a vast forest, we meet Huttser and his mate Palla, two alpha wolves, the head pair of a wolf pack. The pack is running away from Palla's half sister, Morgra, who has taken control of the Balkar, a large group of fighting wolves that used to protect the free packs. Palla is heavily pregnant. Little does the pack know, but Huttser and Palla are about to become the parents of a she-wolf who is blessed with an ancient power called the Sight. What was a race for one wolf pack to survive suddenly becomes a war between good and evil when the pack learns about a terrifying prophecy.<br />According to an ancient legend that Morgra is bent on fufilling, this she-wolf, Larka, is the key to the enslavement of nature itself...if Larka chooses to join her aunt. <br />David Clemet-Davies' works are always a joy for me to read. His writing is riveting and his views on nature is fastinating. He breathes depth and reality into his characters, especially Morgra and Larka. He created a tragic story behind Morgra's evil which really makes the reader want to sympasize with her. Larka is a bit annoying at first. She cleary doesn't want anything to do with the legend or Morgra. Guilt consumes her when most of her pack is killed off by her aunt's powers, which just gives her more meaning. She is so young when all of this begins, so it's really easy to understand the way she feels. And, eventually, she really matures and grasps her destiny. She deeply believes that love can and will stop the legend. Her faith is touching and unshakable, and it didn't just come out of nowhere. It grew in her heart over time, which enlightens her with depth. <br />David Clemet-Davies gives us a breathtaking view of the life of the wild wolf, and only makes it more colorful with eerie fantasy based loosly on ancient Romanian legends. He clearly did his research before writing.<br />Though this is a fantasy, his characters still feel believable. He is able to give them an amazing amount of feeling while still managing to keep them wolfish. You always remember that you're reading about wolves when you read. The supporting characters are simply wonderful in every sense of the word. <br />This story is long, though, and you do need to kind of force yourself along, but with the great writing and characters, you still want to keep going. The begining is a little slow, but I barely noticed that. <br /></b></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="15969398482267599930" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_163770962" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/163770962" />
    <a title="James Steele" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4470061-james-steele"><img alt="James Steele" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442529634p2/4470061.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/163770962?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Steele" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Steele" href="/user/show/4470061-james-steele">James Steele</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer163770962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17791421891261043994">I’m not sure what to make of this story of telepathic wolves. It feels like the book is cleaved unevenly in two, so 2/3 of it is bad and the last 1/3 is enjoyable.<br/><br/>In this first cleave, the dialogue is horrible, the narration describes too little and action is glazed over so quickly it left me wondering did something just happen? In a single paragraph as many as three character’s points of view are given, and this flip-flopping changes the story’s focus so much that it’s hard to get a grip on wha</span>
  <span id="freeText17791421891261043994" style="display:none">I’m not sure what to make of this story of telepathic wolves. It feels like the book is cleaved unevenly in two, so 2/3 of it is bad and the last 1/3 is enjoyable.<br /><br />In this first cleave, the dialogue is horrible, the narration describes too little and action is glazed over so quickly it left me wondering did something just happen? In a single paragraph as many as three character’s points of view are given, and this flip-flopping changes the story’s focus so much that it’s hard to get a grip on what’s happening.<br /><br />But when I crossed into the last third of the book, much of the bad dialogue, polluting adverbs (he wrote <i>suddenly</i>), confusing POV flipping, and hazy descriptions disappeared and what followed was a surprisingly well-written ending. It was like watching a writer’s style mature as he wrote.<br /><br />The ending is exciting, but the beginning and middle are so badly written that the meaning is lost. If the author had taken the first 2/3 through another draft to clarify the enormous amount of back story and get rid of the POV flipping, then it would match the ending and make sense.<br /><br />Honestly, it felt like this could’ve been great. My hat’s off to Mr. Clement-Davies for creating a potentially mind-blowing story, but coming just short of bringing it into the light.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17791421891261043994" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1725327543" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1725327543" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1143969-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1409947508p2/1143969.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1725327543?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/1143969-julie">Julie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=buddy-or-group-reads">buddy-or-group-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=recced">recced</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=talking-animals">talking-animals</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=werewolves">werewolves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1725327543" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6258019701374992085">Buddy-read with Ashley & Stephanie! I remember really-really loving <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58087.Fire_Bringer' title='Fire Bringer by David Clement-Davies' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fire Bringer</a>, which is what led to reading this book (plus WOLVES, I love wolves). I thinnnnnk Clement-Davies' earlier book was better, but my memories are so hazy that it's hard to tell whether it's just nostalgia colouring my view.<br/><br/>Ultimately, I'm going with 3 stars for this: it could go up for some aspects, could go down for others, so I'm resting firmly middle-of-the-line.<br/><br/>Clement-Davies' worldbuilding is really the best part </span>
  <span id="freeText6258019701374992085" style="display:none">Buddy-read with Ashley &amp; Stephanie! I remember really-really loving <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58087.Fire_Bringer" title="Fire Bringer by David Clement-Davies" rel="nofollow noopener">Fire Bringer</a>, which is what led to reading this book (plus WOLVES, I love wolves). I thinnnnnk Clement-Davies' earlier book was better, but my memories are so hazy that it's hard to tell whether it's just nostalgia colouring my view.<br /><br />Ultimately, I'm going with 3 stars for this: it could go up for some aspects, could go down for others, so I'm resting firmly middle-of-the-line.<br /><br />Clement-Davies' worldbuilding is really the best part of the whole book. I really enjoy his vision of wolf society and their belief systems; the world of <u>The Sight</u> is steeped in Christian theology, Norse and Greek mythology, Transylvanian/Balkan history, fairytales, and Dracula(!). Even a nod towards Arthurian myth, too, because "Morgra" is reminiscent of Morgan le Fay/Morgana, complete with her black raven familiar and dark magic (she channelled serious Disney Villainess vibes in pretty much every single one of her scenes). And I'm spoiling the moment, but one of my "oh gosh, this is wonderful" reactions was when one wolf told a tale that you eventually recognise as Cain/Abel, seen through the lens of a reversed Red Riding Hood.<br /><br />But... Unfortunately, at almost 500 pages, this book is just too long and wordy for what it is. The language is relatively complicated, but imo it could/should have been a much faster, punchier, edited-down read, especially since the plot itself is pretty surface-level and straightforward. I just kept thinking how much I want to see this as a kids' animated movie instead (along the lines of <u>Balto</u> or something. just wreck me now!), especially since the cheesy plot might work better in animated form rather than text.<br /><br />I wish I'd read this back when it came out, because then it probably would have been my EVERYTHING. Today, though, it hasn't aged all that well, mostly because everything is SO MELODRAMATIC. The villainess practically cackles "I'll get you, my pretty!" as lightning cracks overhead (I laughed out loud at that scene because it was 2 much).<br /><br />I've also grown much, much less tolerant of Chosen One narratives and prophecies, because they feel like predictably ticking off the boxes, events unfolding the way they were always meant to unfold, and it strips characters of their agency. Instead, my favourite thing is characters <i>railing</i> against their fates and prophecies and thus inadvertently shooting themselves in the foot and bringing about their own downfall (holla, Oedipus). I do really appreciate that the Chosen One was the female wolf, though, and that she was our heroine, when it so easily could have been her brother Fell or friend Kar.<br /><br />It was an enjoyable enough read, but felt like it went veeeery slowly for my usual pace. I wouldn't actively recommend it to people, but I didn't <i>dislike</i> it either, so it really just falls right down the middle (the worldbuilding still being the main redeeming factor). I'll probably still pick up the sequel at some point, but not immediately!<br /><br /><b>Random points:</b><br />- Putting on the obligatory disclaimer: <a href="http://knowledgenuts.com/2014/01/11/the-alpha-wolfe-is-an-outdated-myth/" rel="nofollow noopener">the concept of alpha and beta wolves is a wrong and outdated myth</a>! We should all stop propagating it in our fiction!<br /><br />- It's not <i>actually</i> a werewolf book, but the legends of the Man Varg are rampant enough that I'm shelving it as such anyway.<br /><br />- They namedropped Rannoch from <u>Fire Bringer</u>! And I think he may have cameoed towards the end there??? (I mean, I'm assuming it was him, but I honestly can't remember if he survived the events of his book.)<br /><br /><b>Spoilery notes about the ending:</b><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[- I really like that last page and got feelsy about the final lines, and how it all circled back again and echoed theology some more.<br /><br />- I liked the environmental message while it was happening (an important thing for kid readers to hear about!), but... what was actually the point of the plot in this entire book? If the wolves are already forgetting Larka's vision, then why did all this happen? How are the wolves going to help bring about a breed of psychic environmentalist humans (???)? Is it all on Bran? In that case, was this all technically a prelude to a strapping male human Chosen One saving the world? They stopped Morgra, sure, but that just doesn't feel Significant Enough as an achievement for me, idk.<br /><br />- I honestly cannot decide how I feel about Larka's death. On the one hand: hooray for authors being brave enough to kill their main characters and stick with it! (I had a fleeting moment of being SO AFRAID that Clement-Davies would, to be crass, pussy out and find a way out of it, like too many authors have done with Foretold Deaths. I am sick and tired of Chosen Ones dying and then coming back.) On the other hand, I'm not especially enamoured with stories where our female protagonist has to sacrifice herself for everyone else because she's too good, too pure for this world. On the other other hand, she's their Jesus figure, so it's par for the course? Ugh, I dunno.<br /><br />- The ~twist~ about Wolfbane was the most blatantly telegraphed, obvious reveal that I feel like could only take middle grade readers by surprise. (Then again, I was primed by knowing that the second book in the series is called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1161439.Fell__The_Sight___2_" title="Fell (The Sight, #2) by David Clement-Davies" rel="nofollow noopener">Fell</a>... <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="6258019701374992085" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1197750493" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1197750493" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36164879-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572587100p2/36164879.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1197750493?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/36164879-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36164879-jane?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36164879-jane?shelf=animal">animal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36164879-jane?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1197750493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer616807364233797193"><b>Did Not Finish</b><br/><br/><br/>Sometimes after reading a book this is you, "OMG, what were they talking about? The book was freaking AWESOME!!!" And other times you're like, "Wow, I really wish I had listened to those hate comments." <--- That's me. Bad writing, fake wolf behavior, confusing, just to name a few problems.<br/><br/>Let me get two things straight first, I LOVE wolves, and I LOVE fantasy. So why wouldn't I LOVE this book? Let me tell you why I didn't with many examples.<br/><br/><br/><b>Fake Wolf Behavior:</b><br/><br/><i>As Bran padded inside</i></span>
  <span id="freeText616807364233797193" style="display:none"><b>Did Not Finish</b><br /><br /><br />Sometimes after reading a book this is you, "OMG, what were they talking about? The book was freaking AWESOME!!!" And other times you're like, "Wow, I really wish I had listened to those hate comments." &lt;--- That's me. Bad writing, fake wolf behavior, confusing, just to name a few problems.<br /><br />Let me get two things straight first, I LOVE wolves, and I LOVE fantasy. So why wouldn't I LOVE this book? Let me tell you why I didn't with many examples.<br /><br /><br /><b>Fake Wolf Behavior:</b><br /><br /><i>As Bran padded inside, he pushed accidentally against Huttser, who turned and snarled at him. Bran jumped sideways, creeping back to the edge of the wall in submission and showing his throat to the Dragga.</i><br /><br />Showing his throat? Seriously? Shouldn't it say he rolled onto his back, which is the highest position of submission? No? Okay, how about he pushed his ears back, another sign of submission? No? Alright then... wolves do not show their throat in submission. Another example below.<br /><br /><i>Wolves rarely look at each other directly in the eye for fear they might raise the other's anger.</i><br /><br />First off, wolves are all about eye contact! That is one of the main ways they communicate. Yes, sometimes eye contact can be used to give a warning glare, but it's not usual among pack mates. The author did not do his research at all.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Confusing Parts:</b><br /><br />Throughout the book the wolves use words from their own language, such as Dragga and Drappa. At first I'm thinking this is pretty cool, then we get introduced to Sikla, Varg, Putnar, and Herla. That's a lot of words to remember what they all mean. But no problem, right? Surely the author will explain them a lot so we can catch on. Yeah, no. We get a single explanation for each word, and then we're expected to remember it forever. Awesome.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Cringe Parts:</b><br /><br /><i>"They're so very little, Huttser."</i><br /><i>"I know, Palla."</i><br /><i>The parents fell silent for both knew the harsh laws of survival and how much danger faces young cubs in the world.</i><br /><i>"Well, we shall have to feed you up, Palla," said Huttser tenderly at last, "so your milk is as rich as sunlight."</i><br /><i>"We'll bury the others outside the den, Huttser, beneath the birch tree."</i><br /><br />Am I the only one who was annoyed by the constant use of names within the speech? At first it was cheesy, now it's just annoying.<br /><br /><br /><b>Did I Mention That The Sight Is A Freaking History Book?</b><br /><br /><i>Huttser was right, for this was the time before the coming of Stephen the Great, the Hungarian king and fabled defender of Christendom, when a new threat had arisen like a specter in the East. Even now Ottoman Turks, who came from the southern lands and adhered to nothing of the Christians' beliefs, pressed hard at the haunches of eastern Europe and belief fought belief, as power wrestled power.</i><br /><br />Someone please tell me how this is relevant to the storyline. PLEASE.<br /><br /><br /><b>The Writing:</b><br /><br />Let's be fair, the author obviously did a good amount of world building. It shows quite plainly from the wolf language to the wolf belief system. The editing was good too, as far as punctuation and grammar goes. As for the actual writing, you might be able to see how bad it is from my examples.<br /><br />I don't think I made it far enough into the book to get a really good idea of the storyline, but I think it's mainly about this wolf that is hunting the pack. She is called Morgra and is suppose to have a power called the Sight, although some wolves don't believe in the power. The plot I found very interesting, and I was often wondering about Morgra. The author did a great job at creating suspense, only for the reasons above was the book ruined for me.<br /><br /><br /><b>End Notes:</b><br /><br />I really can't understand how this book ever got to an average of 4 stars. Nonetheless, I will probably come back to this book to see if my opinion of it changed. I doubt it will, but I am willing to give it another shot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="616807364233797193" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TeaDrinkingCat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TeaDrinkingCat" href="/user/show/4965293-teadrinkingcat">TeaDrinkingCat</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer198107256&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer198107256" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7721721564049045571">The Sight is a very unique and original book, I'm sure there are no books like it.<br/><br/>THE GOOD<br/><br/>-The characters. They all have their own personality, and the author makes sure to always follow the character's "rules".<br/>-The ending. The ending just seemed to raw and true, as if it happened somewhere. As if it was based off a real event.<br/>-The deaths. Everyone loves a happy ending, but sometimes they get old. I found it was refreshing how most of the wolves died, but a new generation was born in their honor</span>
  <span id="freeText7721721564049045571" style="display:none">The Sight is a very unique and original book, I'm sure there are no books like it.<br /><br />THE GOOD<br /><br />-The characters. They all have their own personality, and the author makes sure to always follow the character's "rules".<br />-The ending. The ending just seemed to raw and true, as if it happened somewhere. As if it was based off a real event.<br />-The deaths. Everyone loves a happy ending, but sometimes they get old. I found it was refreshing how most of the wolves died, but a new generation was born in their honor.<br />-The Sight. It was...amazing how detailed the rules and ways of the Sight were.<br /><br />THE BAD<br /><br />-Kipcha's death. I mean, I know she could have made it out alive. And then it was revealed that she killed herself, because she wouldn't be able to raise pups without a father. I mean, wouldn't you want to keep the pups alive, for Khaz?<br />-Slavka. I just...didn't like her character. She had horrible mood swings, and at sometimes I had thought it was a typo when they said her name. She was probably the only character that didn't seem true.<br />-Wolfbane. I did not understand the whole, "Fell being Wolfbane" thing. I mean, if he didn't die, wouldn't he remember his family? Or did Morgra just brainwash him? It was confusing.<br /><br /><br />Overall, it's a great read, but near the middle it gets slow, and I found myself skipping paragraphs. But, it's nice wen you want a break from the average fantasy-animal books.</span>

<div id="review_926572005" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/926572005" />
    <a title="Stephanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5975841-stephanie"><img alt="Stephanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469205584p2/5975841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/926572005?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie" href="/user/show/5975841-stephanie">Stephanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780525467236" href="/book/show/1934811.The_Sight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5975841-stephanie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5975841-stephanie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5975841-stephanie?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5975841-stephanie?shelf=anthropomorphism">anthropomorphism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer926572005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10486736607702172">   The last time I read this book, I was in high school (about 10 years ago (!) ), and I remember really enjoying it – a story about a wolf family, with magic and a prophecy to guide the way. And that there was a cameo from Clement-Davies other book, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58087.Fire_Bringer' title='Fire Bringer by David Clement-Davies' rel='nofollow noopener'> Fire Bringer</a>. The rest of the details I was fuzzy at best on. This time, it is a buddy-read with Julie and Ashley!<br/><br/>   I will say, this book reads a lot longer than it should – there is a lot of depth to the story, with many different elements tied i</span>
  <span id="freeText10486736607702172" style="display:none">   The last time I read this book, I was in high school (about 10 years ago (!) ), and I remember really enjoying it – a story about a wolf family, with magic and a prophecy to guide the way. And that there was a cameo from Clement-Davies other book, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/58087.Fire_Bringer" title="Fire Bringer by David Clement-Davies" rel="nofollow noopener"> Fire Bringer</a>. The rest of the details I was fuzzy at best on. This time, it is a buddy-read with Julie and Ashley!<br /><br />   I will say, this book reads a lot longer than it should – there is a lot of depth to the story, with many different elements tied in to create a certain atmosphere – elements and reflections of Christianity, of Roman mythology, of Carpathian/Romanian mythology, even allusions to a certain fanged creature of the dark. There are definitely parallels drawn, too, between not only the wolf mythology of Tor and Fenris with their daughter Sita’s sacrifice for the wolves to Jesus in Christianity (right down to small details, such as “[Sita] said that they should send her their children, for she knew that children can really see the truth and she loved children above all things” – page 74), but this parallel is extended even further into the active characters of this story, namely, Larka. I found the parallels very obvious, though you were not quite hit over the head with them despite the repetition (especially with how very closely Sita’s story reflects the life and death of Jesus… still can’t quite get over how her story is basically a copy of Jesus’ story).<br /><br />   Another theme which played out just as loud as, if not louder than, the Sita/Jesus/character parallels was a theme of fear, and the evolution of the perception/reception of fear. Some examples of that evolution:<br /><br />   <b>“Fear is an instinct, like hunger or anger. We need it to help us survive, and it is nothing to be ashamed of. It tells us whether we should fight or flee.”—page 162<br /><br />   “I love the wilderness, Slavka,” [Huttser] growled, “but it is hard too.”<br />    “Yes, Huttser,” she agreed, “as we must be hard. There must be no place for weakness or fear, for fear destroys thought. We must be strong, strong as the wilderness itself. […]” – page 260<br /><br />   [Larka said,] “We must live free from fear, Jarla. Nothing can grow in fear, but hate.” – page 281</b><br /><br />   There were more instances/moments talking and referring to fear and its benefits and detriments in life, but the above were some of the ones which stuck out the most for me. I found the evolution and changes in how the characters perceived fear interesting, because just as we do in real life, there would be back and forths about the benefits and the detriments of how much one lets fear affect one’s life, and there was a certain fluidity with the resultant effects of these shifting views.<br /><br />   As I mentioned earlier, this book was also a lot longer than it really needed to be to tell its story. There were several contributing factors to this – the extended parallels between Larka’s story and wolf/human lore/religion, the nature of a vague prophecy and how it ends up being followed or rather how it ends up happening regardless of how invested the characters are in helping it to come true. Part of it was also what I found a strange balance of when scenes are told to the reader versus shown to the reader – there were some instances where events were roughly summarized which I think would have been better detailed, and other instances where we were given a play-by-play of some event which seemed less important and worthy of such detail. One example of this is <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<i>It was the rebels who had captured them on the ice that night […] At first Huttser had asked for free passage through the mountains, but Slavka had laughed at him and told him that the free wolves were either for or against Morgra. That there was no middle way.</i> – page 255 – there is a lot of potential in this interaction, which will set the stage for how future interactions between Huttser/Palla and Slavka play out. But we only get the summary of their first encounter, and thus miss out on seeing that crucial first interaction with all of its nuances, with Huttser/Palla hiding who they are from Slavka while still having to play at her game in order to survive. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />   Contributing to the length of the book, though in a good way, were some of the descriptions of the natural world scattered throughout. From the very first page of the book, we get a crystal clear idea of how this world appears, and the beauty of it all. And these descriptions continue to be scattered throughout the book: <br /><br />   <b> At last they came to the far edge of the trees, and the river lay before them. It was at its widest point here. They saw a perfect sheet of white stretching ahead and glistening brilliantly in the moonlight. Along the banks, willow and vine trees had bowed their heads over the frozen edge of the water, and their trailing branches were shawled with snow. Below them, the last residues of autumn grasses—strangling leaves and tilting bulrushes—had been seized into a static beauty by winter’s grip, glittering with tiny icicles and bulbs of frozen dew that flashed like stars.</b> – page 167-168<br /><br />   Clement-Davies definitely does an excellent job <i>painting</i> the world of <u>The Sight</u>, and infuses life and color and energy into even the coldest, darkest of scenes.<br /><br />   Instead of rambling on about more aspects of the story, some of which are addressed in the quotes/commentary section below anyways, I will say that it was a nice read, though a simple, rather straight-forward story cloaked in complex images and allusions. I’ll read the sequel, though I hope it will better address one thing in particular that sort of bothered me about Larka – how she just accepted her role, rather slipped into it, with ease despite her misgivings and initial desire to have nothing to do with it – it was rather a case of the prophecy left her no choice, and she decided to accept that she had no choice and just go along with it after several trials even though she could have still railed against her “destiny” more actively. I hope that the sequel will delve more into the MC’s head and thoughts, so we get a clearer picture of the evolution of their decisions and preferences than we did with Larka.<br /><br />Quotes/commentary/questions:<br /><br />    [Brassa explained,] “She’s ready, Huttser, and she must do this alone. The law allows no Varg into the den during birthing. Don’t worry. Palla will summon you when she’s ready.” – page 25 – I found it kind of strange that it would be required she give birth alone, without even the elderly/experienced Brassa by her side to help…<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[    [Khaz’s secret that] Khaz’s cubs had been stirring inside her. The cubs that at first she had fought the river to protect, until she remembered that they would never have a father to love and nurture them. – page 122 – I found it kind of strange that Kipcha would just…give up like this without Khaz to father their cubs; I mean, her and Khaz and Brassa and Bran all contributed to raising Larka and Fell, so why wouldn’t the pack take over care of Kipcha’s cubs as well? Does this go back to how only the Dragga and Drappa of the pack are supposed to have cubs in this particular wolf family-structure present in this book? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />    “And do not dreams tell us truths and secrets of the world before we recognize things with our waking thoughts?” – page 217<br /><br />    “[…] The body can grow sick with guilt and shame and secrets. Or even by taking on too much responsibility.” – page 245<br /><br />   <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[“But here,” said Karma almost sadly, her growls reaching deeper and deeper, “Slavka will not let us talk of such things, or even tell our own stories. Not of Zostar, nor Tor, nor the Sight. She says we must believe nothing of faith, old or new, but only in fighting and survival. She thinks they are the same as freedom.”<br />    […] “But don’t you agree?” asked Palla quietly.<br />    “What freedom is it to believe in nothing?” snorted Karma.<br />    “But, Karma,” whispered Keeka, looking even more confused, “Palla believes in Tor and Fenris. While you believe in Zostar. They can’t both be true. That stands to reason.”<br />    “They may just be stories,” nodded Karma, “ways of naming and talking about the world. But my kind believe that in stories often great truths lie concealed, unconscious truths, if we only know how to interpret them.”<br />[truth a word for that which is not a lie – What about the slavery of knowing too much?] – page 266 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />    “[…] Enjoy your form, Larka, for you have a right to be what you are, as much right as anything else.” – page 293<br /><br />   <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Page 295 – when Larka is far away and sees danger coming towards her new “pack,” she complains that the Sight is useless because she can’t use it to help/help warn them. But couldn’t she slip into Skart, even briefly, so that she could relay him the message?<br /><br />   Everywhere, the rebels lay: at the edge of the trees, contorted into unnatural shapes, some still stirring as the life left them, others alive only in the energy that decay would return to the earth. – page 328<br /><br />   Kar had lost the living pool of company in which to view his own reflection. He no longer knew himself or what he was becoming. – page 360 – Seems a little strange how this came about, but okay…<br /><br />   Larka nodded slowly. “You must try and forget,” she whispered, “and forgive. That is the only way to be free, Gart. Or at least forget the hate your memories bring. We must learn to heal our memories.” – page 394<br /><br />   Standing alone above the tree line, on a rocky outcrop, was a single red deer. – page 402 -- …Rannoch?!?! How could that even be possible, unless it is a vision for Larka maybe?<br /><br />    […] in that moment, he had known that to kill the body was not the most terrible thing there was, but to kill and maim the soul with hate and lies, to kill the mind and heart that must be free. – page 435 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />   Because love takes responsibility, and in all experience, too, there is a pact between the seer and the seen, the listener and the storyteller, the judge and the judged. – page 448<br /><br />   <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[“I did not escape the legend, Kar. For it was its own kind of trap, as Man’s freedom will be if he doesn’t learn. But life is not a legend or a story. Reality is far more precious than a story. And to love one another we must begin to see each other properly. Besides, Kar, at times, the greatest courage of all is to live.” – page 459 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br />Typos:<br />“The cave was low ceilinged, so that Huttser… -- page 13 – Opening quote unnecessary, as no one is speaking.<br /><br />…share the secrets she was learning about the sight. – page 184 – “sight” should be capitalized, as it refers to the Sight.<br /><br />…just as Larka had once done in the den Larka snarled at the sight. – page 220 – Could be just a printing error, but there should be a period after “den”.<br /><br />… the [three] wolves panting breath steaming and smoking around the baby… -- page 277 I think it should be “wolves’ “? <br /><br />…fondly at her parents “I must leave you now […]” – page 368 – Missing a period after “parents”<br /><br />…nuzzling the little Sikla toward him… -- page 463 – But previously, she had been referred to as a drappa…</span>
  <a data-text-id="10486736607702172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1577527727" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1577527727" />
    <a title="Pandora Elinor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53653551-pandora-elinor"><img alt="Pandora Elinor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1554222908p2/53653551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1577527727?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pandora Elinor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pandora Elinor" href="/user/show/53653551-pandora-elinor">Pandora Elinor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330483858" href="/book/show/1401981.The_Sight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53653551-pandora-elinor?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53653551-pandora-elinor?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1577527727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer518401899457304262">I loved this book as a child. I'm not sure how old I was but it really captured my imagination. Now that I've reread it I can see why: first of all the themes and setting are amazing. Wolves in Transylvania, wild forests and mountains, snow and ice. A prophecy, a young white she-wolf with the Sight. Awesome! <br/><br/>And the author definitely has a gift for creating stunning, awe-inspiring imagery. It really fires up the imagination. The deserted citadel, the black wolf and the white wolf, the wheeling e</span>
  <span id="freeText518401899457304262" style="display:none">I loved this book as a child. I'm not sure how old I was but it really captured my imagination. Now that I've reread it I can see why: first of all the themes and setting are amazing. Wolves in Transylvania, wild forests and mountains, snow and ice. A prophecy, a young white she-wolf with the Sight. Awesome! <br /><br />And the author definitely has a gift for creating stunning, awe-inspiring imagery. It really fires up the imagination. The deserted citadel, the black wolf and the white wolf, the wheeling eagle... The prophecy feels tangible and inescapable as well all through the book, as I read I was hooked on finding out how it would unfold and come to pass. The worldbuilding and mythology are complex and clearly the author put a lot of thought into them.<br /><br />So before I get to my criticisms I do want to make it clear that this book does get some things right, and for those reasons alone it's already an interesting, worthwhile read. But I feel I need to point out where it fails for me, because these things could also bother other people. So this will probably sound really negative, even though in reality I'm of two minds over this book. I really enjoyed some aspects, it's just that others were problematic for me.<br /><br />First of all, a couple minor details: the characterisation isn't very layered. There is a large cast of characters but most are very one-dimensional, making it hard to feel attached to any of them. This book takes an even more extreme approach to character safety than A Song of Ice and Fire, yet I didn't feel as sad as I should have because I just didn't identify with many characters.<br />I would say the only ones I really liked and had an emotional connection with were Larka, who is a lovely character, brave when it counts but flawed and likeable, and Kar, who is just a darling. <br />One character we were supposed to like, Huttser, was even actively unlikeable to me. He was a macho jerk throwing his weight around.<br /><br />The dialogue wasn't the best. At times it felt stilted and unnatural, or was really too obviously a way to get some exposition in. The characters kept telling each other stories, and while I get that the author was trying to build a mythology for his world, some of them were rather unnecessary and felt overindulgent.<br /><br />There is a lot of head-hopping. I am probably more patient than most about this, but even to me it got a little jarring sometimes. But mostly I could get over it. However one thing that annoyed me, I don't know why, is when in the midst of the head hopping some really clumsy foreshadowing would happen. Let me give an example : say two characters have just had an emotionally charged conversation, they turn tail and leave. The narration will then say something like "But even as they left, they didn't know eyes were glinting from behind the leaves, watching them". This happens all. the. damn. time and it got irritating. If you want to use foreshadowing, find a way of weaving it naturally into the story. Don't keep pointing out what your characters don't know, it makes them seem stupid and doesn't help us feel connected to them.<br /><br />Lastly there was clearly an attempt to weave human history in the region, and even human history in general, into the story. I don't feel like the author quite pulled this off. As he was trying to write in the wolf worldview, he would try to simplify all of this into concepts that wolves could maybe understand, except that was a failure and only succeeded in making this history seem dumbed down, sometimes overly general and sometimes weirdly overly specific, as well as oriented and heavy handed about his message. You could tell he was trying to make this into timeless wisdom and... it just didn't work for me.<br /><br />Now let's get to the major problems, for me. First, my pet peeve, animal behaviour. While some of the mistakes could be attributed to out-of-date references, this book is just far too inaccurate about wolf behaviour and pack dynamics. Writing anthropomorphic fantasy must be really difficult and I am willing to allow for minor inaccuracies, as long as the spirit of the animal, the fundamental differences from human worldview, are respected. I didn't really feel that was the case here. Wolves gathering in packs of male fighters, or gathering in a huge army-like pack and training for war... No. That's just impossible to imagine when you know anything about this species' behaviour. The composition of the pack of heroes we are introduced to was also all wrong, as far as my understanding of wolves go. There were also some huge mistakes that had me scratching my head. Wolves don't sweat until their fur is soaked. They don't cry tears. Etc. A lot of this could have been corrected with a little research. I don't feel there was enough of an effort to enter into the potential mindset of a wolf. There was good, imaginative worldbuilding. There just wasn't enough effort to make it match up with reality.<br /><br />Next, and perhaps most egregious of all to me: the sexism. Funny how I hadn't noticed any of this as a child, but during this reread I had to check the publication date at one stage because I just couldn't believe a modern book could be this bad. 2001. I felt like it could have been 40 years older. It's doubly disappointing to me because none of this actually exists in the wild between wolves. There really wasn't any need for any of it. This is 100% the author inserting his worldview and his prejudice about males and females into the story. The author thinks the alpha male is the ultimate leader of the pack, and when it comes down to it, his mate must obey him. (This stupid idea is a large part of why Huttser comes across as such an ass. I don't even think the author realises how much of a jerk he's made poor Huttser, in order to conform to his idea of an alpha male). It's his right to take the cubs on their first hunt, as his mate reminds him when she cedes the role to him. Males will hunt for the pack while all 3 females stay behind to look after a measly 2 cubs. Because those are their roles. Anyone with a little knowledge about wolves will know how absurd this is. Both baddies in the story are females, and in both cases became big baddies because of a perversion, a wrongness, in their performance of motherhood, the ultimate feminine role. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Morgra is sterile. It is repeatedly thrown in her face that she cannot experience a mother's love for her cubs. She is bitter and filled with hate. Slavka preferred to kill her own cubs than to let human hunters and their dogs kill them. This has turned her "hard" and now she pursues an unnatural militaristic idea of survival. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> There are no true male baddies, somehow. Lastly, even as a child I was a little bothered that Larka, who has had a more powerful and active role than other good females are allowed in the story, has to <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[die at the end. Only the males who have shared her extraordinary fate are also allowed to continue on with a normal life. She cannot have both power and knowledge, and also live to become a mother and raise cubs. She must die, a virgin sacrifice. Somehow doesn't sit well with me. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />Anyway, I'm not good at articulating exactly how and why but some very antiquated attitudes permeate the story and kind of ruin it for me. I can sometimes enjoy some works despite some sexism in them. But I think in this case it's particularly jarring because it really is human projection and not something that actually exists among wolves.<br /><br />Lastly, this story is so unrelentingly dark and hopeless. It pays lip service to love and hope but we really don't get to see much of those. It's a lot of death, of sadness and fear. (GRRM stares at this book's death toll in envy!) Somehow I was more ok with this as a kid. Maybe because now I've seen how hard life can be, I don't really want more of that in my stories and I prefer to read books where there is more balance between pain and joy. I can read dark things, but there needs to be a bit of humour and happiness too to keep me going. The end is... unsatisfying. It feels meaningless. I don't understand WHY this had to happen, why it was inevitable, why the price had to be paid. It won't have made any difference.<br /><br />The sexism and the bleak, dark tone are two main reasons I wouldn't recommend this for a kid. However if you are passionate about wolves, or like atmospheric stories about dark prophecies, it's worth a read.</span>

    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19976423-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487742988p2/19976423.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/19976423-anna">Anna</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer6953089518340797882">This is the second time I've tried to read this book after having bought it at like 13, but alas, it was not to be as I've decided to drop this book again at 50% completion.<br/><br/>Firstly, I found the overtly Christian undertones really off-putting. Davies basically copies stories straight from the Bible and substitutes in wolf characters, including a wolf Jesus, a wolf God, a wolf Adam and Eve and a even Wolf Satan. Now, I understand writers hope to give their readers a message in their works, but I l</span>
  <span id="freeText6953089518340797882" style="display:none">This is the second time I've tried to read this book after having bought it at like 13, but alas, it was not to be as I've decided to drop this book again at 50% completion.<br><br>Firstly, I found the overtly Christian undertones really off-putting. Davies basically copies stories straight from the Bible and substitutes in wolf characters, including a wolf Jesus, a wolf God, a wolf Adam and Eve and a even Wolf Satan. Now, I understand writers hope to give their readers a message in their works, but I like <i>my</i> subliminal messages to be a little more subtle than having literal direct-quotes force fed to me, <i>and</i> I like them to include a hearty dose of Atheism. <br>The frequent <i>Sunday-school-esque</i> scenes where the children asked questions about the God and Jesus wolf and discussed "free will"were pure agony and felt patronising to me, hence why I think this book was aimed at a younger, more influenceable target audience. <br><br>With that in mind, there is a frightening glamorisation of suicidal thoughts, and sexist and classist implications. I think Davies used the fact that his characters were wolves to pretend they, and therefore his writing, were outside human morals and rules. Like, of <i>course</i> he gets physical with her and constantly snaps to put her in her place, he's the <i>Alpha.</i><br><br>The plot progression occurred one of two ways.<br><b>1) The characters' completely needless stupidity.</b><br>For example, when crossing a river covered in very thin ice, after having been <i>explicitly</i> warned to look out for the "fifth element" ice(?) <br> <blockquote>
<i>"Safety," whispered Palla. "Safety at last."<br>But as Fell brought up the rear, and the wolves grew more confident, he found himself looking up into the night sky again. His attention began to wander as he gazed at the stars. </i>The Wolf Trail, the pathway between heaven and earth, <i>he kept thinking, and as he did so, he started to drift away to the right.</i>
</blockquote><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439579751i/15856322.jpg" width="300" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>You have to pay attention for all of five minutes to <b>NOT DIE!</b><br><br><b>2) Overhearing strangers that show up out of no-where.</b><br>In the 223 pages I read, they learned what to do next this way <b>four, different, times.</b><br>Four times! He clearly kept hitting dead ends since he went and killed off all the decision making characters with their own idiocy to generate some drama!<br>Also, lets not forget, this is a book about wild animals, yet <i>somehow</i> our heroes hide within earshot of wolves, <b> with noses</b> and somehow go undetected <i>every time?</i><br><br>I think the final straw for me was when he used a cop-out 'technique' I find more infuriating than any other, which is when no proper explanation is given when a character asks "Why didn't you say something before?"<br>A prime example of this is in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1162543.Breaking_Dawn__Twilight___4_" title="Breaking Dawn (Twilight, #4) by Stephenie Meyer" rel="nofollow noopener">Breaking Dawn</a> when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Edward says that Jacob being a 'shape shifter', not a werewolf <i>all along</i> <b>NEVER CAME UP!?!?!?</b> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Basically, it goes a little something like this:<br><blockquote>
  <i>"Then why didn't he talk to me before?" asked Larka, suddenly feeling bitterness again for all that had happened to her. "if he'd helped us before perhaps <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Kar- <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>-"<br>"Don't be ungrateful," Snapped Skart. "I saved your life, didn't I? Before you were far too young, Larka. Your eye wasn't open yet and I didn't want to frighten you away. Though if I'd realized quite how much you know already, perhaps I might have come even sooner. But anyway, I needed to wait."<br>"Wait for what?" said Larka sullenly.<br>"Wait for you to ask for help, of course." </i>
</blockquote><br>Oh, <b>of course</b>. I watched you struggle all along <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ as everyone around you died <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and never let you know I was even here, but <b>of course</b> all you had to do was <i>ask</i>.<br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
