<div id="review_3494106507" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3494106507" />
    <a title="Jessie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/118809260-jessie"><img alt="Jessie" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3494106507?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessie" href="/user/show/118809260-jessie">Jessie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/118809260-jessie?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3494106507" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5858863427782840867"><i>
  <b>As a she-cub is whelped with a coat that is white, / And a human child stolen to suckle the Sight..... </b>
</i> <br/><br/>This is probably the most difficult review I've had to ever put together for any book, and that's because I'm trying to mesh my childhood enchantment at this book with more critical analysis. <br/><br/>I'm going to skip telling the plot, if only because a hundred reviews have done so before me. Needless to say, it's a drama between talking wolves, with an evil she-wolf whose plans involve taking ove</span>
  <span id="freeText5858863427782840867" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>As a she-cub is whelped with a coat that is white, / And a human child stolen to suckle the Sight..... </b>
</i> <br><br>This is probably the most difficult review I've had to ever put together for any book, and that's because I'm trying to mesh my childhood enchantment at this book with more critical analysis. <br><br>I'm going to skip telling the plot, if only because a hundred reviews have done so before me. Needless to say, it's a drama between talking wolves, with an evil she-wolf whose plans involve taking over the wolf military and ruling the wolves with an iron fist (paw), while chasing her sister's pack to the ends of the earth. She has a fledgling magical power, known as the stuff of myth and legend in wolf culture, and she is looking for another just like her as was foretold. Together, they can unwind reality itself....unless a sacrifice is paid.<br><br>The thing is, <i>The Sight</i> is many things to many people. For some, it's a classic. For others, it profoundly misses the mark. <br><br><b>However</b>, I found this Gothic tale - that's what it is, full of European settings, castles, dark religion, temples, blood, ravens, empires, prophecies and, well, wolves - to be marvellous. A cursed family, fleeing the inevitability of fate amidst a beautiful setting -what a beginning! It reads like an old epic, and has features not dissimilar from an opera or one of the Narnia novels. So much so, in fact, that as a child I used to carry this book everywhere. And I mean everywhere.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597595263ra/29976436.gif" width="500" height="251" alt="Moon with clouds across it." class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Perhaps that is the reason why it doesn't quite ring true for some readers - because ultimately it's hoist by its own petard. If it had been published next to <i>Dracula</i>, or had been some old text by an author that we barely know anything about, the melodrama would be accepted. The novel appears to have fallen foul of the recent literary fatigue for certain themes and ideas, and that, for the record, isn't its fault. It's a casualty of the current literary thinking and the trends that dominate the book market. I'm not saying it's a part of the Western canon (though, to be honest, that idea is complete and utter bollocks), but I'm saying that this is a book out of time, out of context. <br><br><i>
  <b>With blood at the altar the vision shall come / When the eye of the moon is round as the sun..... </b>
</i><br><br>And that's because <i>everyone</i> in this novel is out of time - or, rather, so engrained in their own as to make even reading it out of its setting seem bizarre and unnatural. I'd kill to be able to read this in the Carpathian Mountains. It's steeped, almost marinated, in traditionalism. Everything is reminiscent of what we have seen before, not in the sense of rehashing old things but more in the idea of revelling in repetition, in ritual, in the established way of things (this is, again, something that goes against the current literary trends). The wolves are a proud race with their own concept of honour, the idea of something ancient to be adhered to. There's a glory to, and a longing for, the old times within every character; there exists a melancholy that can't quite be shifted, a feeling further emphasised by the dramatic irony that, of course, the time of the wolves is coming to an end. Soon, the world will change. This isn't the only source of dramatic irony: the tradition that is so adhered to, the one that the characters prize so much, is, in the end, the very thing that leads to the family's doom. Its cyclical, and more to the point, it traps the reader in the same cycle of remembering and forgetting as its main characters. It is not a spoiler to say the lera, the wild animals, forget. We do too. There is nothing new under the sun. <br><br>Its ideas about prophecy, about magical talking wolves, about this mystical world where mysterious powers intertwine, is in my view pure poetry. It does seem to have aged into some sort of cringy, hackneyed attempt at a plot, and you can see that through other, more brutal reviews of the title; a lot of reviewers see this edge. But I find this book works well when you envelop yourself in its mysticism. <br><br><i>
  <b> In the citadel raised by the lords of before, / The stone twins await-both the power and the law.... </b>
</i><br><br>This whole novel is incredibly allegorical in tone. It's fatalistic, and some may say gloomy, but tragedies remain a source of entertainment - probably because we can explore sad things 'safely' but also, probably, because there's something inherently soothing about a closed circle, about a fate you can't escape. What is going to happen happens, and in that, you can find peace. There's no worrying about what's going to happen. The course is set; the words are written; the friendly jaws of the trap kiss each incision into your skin. It's partially a bildungsroman, partially a meditation on family, and partially....something older. It's incredibly cinematic in its scope. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597595361ra/29976437.gif" width="500" height="284" alt="Wolf running." class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The thing is, it might be easy for some people to dismiss <i>The Sight</i> based on its young fanbase. But not only was I part of that fanbase once upon a time, and it helped me a lot when I was younger and gave me something else to concentrate when times were tough for me - which is valid - but I've said it before, and I'll say it again: <i>just because kids like something doesn't mean it's automatically bad</i>. If anything, I'd say the reason kids seem to love it is because they haven't encountered the same literary influences as adults have. It wades into subject matter that kids haven't quite touched on yet, so it seamlessly fits in with what they read later. As an adult, however, it appears like much of the same. It's a weird kaleidoscopic book where no one sees quite the same picture. <br><br><i>
  <b> And who shall divine, in the dead of the night, / The lies from the truth, the darkness from light?</b>
</i><br><br>Some people have mentioned the lack of accuracy when it comes to wolf behaviour, and probably chiefly the fact that there's the pack 'sikla', or omega (the research that introduced the alpha, beta and omega systems in wolf packs was based on artificial wolves in an unrealistic environment - they're family sets in real life). However, come on - this is entertainment. You can suspend disbelief. I don't think Clement-Davies (god bless his parents for their unoriginality) was <i>trying</i> to write something accurate here. Even my childhood self as an absolute animal fanatic was willing to put my otherwise picky nature aside to enjoy the fiction. It's like the romance genre in that talking animal books are always going to be a niche interest. <br><br> The only reason I don't give it a full five stars is that I'm not blind to its faults. Others have mentioned the sexism of this book, and to be honest although it didn't give me too much grief at the time - I saw it more ritualistic than anything - I can see their point of view. The fact that the female main antagonist is sterile is seen as a perversion, and that definitely hasn't aged well. <br><br>However, if you took the tropes apart, I think more people would like this book. You've got a brother and a sister pair, one doomed to darkness, the other light; a power couple <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[forced to fight one another <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, the 'resistance', persecution, a creepy soothsayer, a quite honestly very cool prophecy poem, doomed lovers, the idea of mystical elements; animal companions; brutality amongst beauty; I could go on. The imagery and symbolism of this book is half the fun. The planets, history, an epic battle, a holy citadel; what more could you want? Clement-Davies gives us a lavish feast for the senses in this novel. It is rich in embroidery, in ornate trappings that all add to the doomed majesty of its characters. This book revels in incorporating all of the primal elements of a fairy tale in a new way, and I wish that half of the readers who enjoy modern fairy tales, and all of the really popular 'reimaginings' that are currently on the market try <i>The Sight</i> first. And again, remember the young fanbase - leave it to the kids. They recognise a fairy tale when they read one. <br><br>I'm persuading myself to read this again now. <br><br><b>Tl;dr</b> - the takeaway is that this book has divided opinion, and will probably continue to do so. I, however, loved it and will continue to love it. Perhaps now is the right time for <i>The Sight</i> to make a return, as it itself did, as it will do, and, I am convinced, as it will, perhaps with a better response.<br><br><b>
  <i>Like the cry of the scavenger, torn through the air / A courage is needed as deep as despair...</i>
</b></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="5858863427782840867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/862897339?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TurquoiseStone" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TurquoiseStone" href="/user/show/1530588-turquoisestone">TurquoiseStone</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer862897339" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer603108554367108737">I decided to give this book two stars instead of one because as a novel about wolves it is something. That being said, aside from the fact the that characters were wolves, The Sight didn't have anything going for it.<br/><br/>First of all the dialogue had issues. I am definitely not a fan of the characters using each-others names in each line of speech, especially when only two characters are talking!<br/><br/>Also Clement-Davies did not do enough research about wolves. Every time he had one of the wolves 'soaked i</span>
  <span id="freeText603108554367108737" style="display:none">I decided to give this book two stars instead of one because as a novel about wolves it is something. That being said, aside from the fact the that characters were wolves, The Sight didn't have anything going for it.<br /><br />First of all the dialogue had issues. I am definitely not a fan of the characters using each-others names in each line of speech, especially when only two characters are talking!<br /><br />Also Clement-Davies did not do enough research about wolves. Every time he had one of the wolves 'soaked in sweat' I wanted to throw the book across the room! Wolves can't sweat, instead, like dogs they pant to release excess body heat. The only sweating they can do is through the pads on their paws.<br /><br />The overall style of the book made reading very cumbersome and slow. I found myself focusing more on the way the book was written instead of what it was trying to say. I came across multiple typos, including one of Morgra's name being spelled Mogra and the line 'Larka's eyes opened as she stared back.', What was the author's intent here? That her eyes widened? Actually, I got the feeling that The Sight was originally written in a different language than English and translated, which would explain the jumbled writing style, but no.<br /><br />As for the plot, I can't help but compare 'The Sight' to 'Watership Down' and I find Clement-Davies book extremely lacking. There's so much he could have done. I would have far more enjoyed the story of the every wolf trying to survive. Not the chosen one, not the chosen family pack, just a average run of the mill wolf. It didn't have to be grand and spectacular. The wolves' legends were also copy paste from Christianity with an extra deity and extremely distracting. of course the author chose it that way so that he could ram home his belief in evolution.<br /><br />Why in all these books about animals must the end moral be against man? One of those, only-you-can-prevent-the-down-fall-of-planet-earth, lessons. We hear that in every single book. I am tried of it! Having characters lament global warming doesn't make me want to recycle and reuse. It makes me want to throw the book across the room!<br /><br />I wish there were more animal centric stories that didn't involve man. Just cut him out all together, or give him a very minor part -That doesn't make the balance of the earth hang in his hands- I read books with animal characters to read about animals, not humans, not global warming, just animals. </span>
  <a data-text-id="603108554367108737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It's far too long, it reads as a rehash of Davies first book, Fire Bringer and the deaths are just awful. I understand keeping things realistic but Davies just takes it a step too far.   <span id="freeText3745604665083327321" style="display:none">Man...what can I say about this book. <br />Last read this nearly a decade ago. 3.5 stars. The story is good but it's greatly flawed. It's far too long, it reads as a rehash of Davies first book, Fire Bringer and the deaths are just awful. I understand keeping things realistic but Davies just takes it a step too far. This book filled me with hopelessness. Fire bringer came full circle and left me feeling contented but The Sight just left me sad and confused. <br /><br />Also all the lore and stories of Gods etc just didn't work for me in this book, they weren't slipped seamlessly into the telling but disrupted the story every time they popped up. So much repetition in this book had my eyes glazing over more than once. <br /><br />For all that you do fall in love with the characters, you become invested in their wellbeing (at your own risk...). The story is interesting and well fleshed out. Some parts of the book are beautiful and interesting. <br /><br />I'm not rushing into the sequel of this book - Fell. I need a couple of books in between to lift my spirits! </span>

<div id="review_300954187" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/300954187" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4552039-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579546503p2/4552039.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/300954187?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/4552039-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142408742" href="/book/show/1634915.The_Sight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4552039-anna?shelf=animals">animals</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer300954187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10724731138226720079">‘The Sight’ is a story of magic and ancient prophecies, of men and beasts, of evil and those resisting it, of spirits and beliefs. But above all of that, it’s a story of one small tight-knit wolf pack. Larka and her brother Fell are born to Palla and Huttser, the alpha pair of a small pack of wolves, but from the very beginning, their lives are in danger.   <span id="freeText10724731138226720079" style="display:none">‘The Sight’ is a story of magic and ancient prophecies, of men and beasts, of evil and those resisting it, of spirits and beliefs. But above all of that, it’s a story of one small tight-knit wolf pack. Larka and her brother Fell are born to Palla and Huttser, the alpha pair of a small pack of wolves, but from the very beginning, their lives are in danger. Vicious she-wolf Morgra that possesses the mysterious Sight had united the packs of Balkar and strives for a dominion above all – and she has her own plans for the two wolf pups. However, Larka also holds the power of the Sight – and she and Fell have a destiny of their own. <br /><br />I have a lot of mixed emotions about this book. It’s a multi-layered story that touches on many topics and explores the characters to their very limits, and while I adored some aspects of it, some of them left me annoyed or frustrated. To start with the upsides, the <b>family dynamics</b> in the story were absolutely marvelous. <b>Larka</b> is the main hero of ‘The Sight’, but her journey would’ve been impossible without her family, and even though there is a smidgen of romance in the story, it takes a backseat next to the family bonds. In the center of it there is a trio of young wolves – Larka, her brother Fell, and their adopted brother Kar. Like that of all siblings, their relationship is not always an easy one, as Fell is often jealous of the attention Larka gets because of her Sight, which prompts him to become reckless and hot-headed, and Kar often feels overshadowed by his strong-willed friends. But ever since they were pups, they made a promise to take care of each other, and even though life tore them apart, they still manage to find a way back to each other, through hardships both physical and mental. <br /> <br />In fact, it’s <b>Kar</b> who quickly became my favourite, even though he doesn’t have as much page time as Larka or Fell. Compared to the siblings, Kar is physically the weakest - not as strong as Fell or as heroic as Larka, and he is submissive by nature, but he is also sensible and smart and loyal, and his ability to keep his head makes him more likeable to me than Fell's temper and snappiness and Larka's mood swings and tendency to blame herself. And even though Kar isn’t the bravest wolf around, he stands fast with his friends when it matters the most, saving Larka's life at least three times throughout the story. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[There is only a hint of romance between him and Larka, but what we get is the sweetest, because the bond between them born from puphood friendship is so strong. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />However, it’s <b>Palla and Huttser</b> who completely won me over as characters and as a couple. It’s unusual to see the hero’s parents given this much attention in the story, yet they not only play an important role in Larka’s life and take part in the ultimate battle against Morgra, but also develop their own plotline, and they really grew on me throughout the book. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Palla and Huttser are not a perfect couple, and they fight bitterly and suffer a nasty break-up after losing their whole pack and one of their children, but eventually, their bonds and loyalty to each other turn out to be so strong that they still defend each other no matter what. To me, a truly awe-inspiring moment came when Slavka’s rebels forced Palla and Huttser to fight each other, claiming that the loser will be killed quickly, but the <i>winner</i> would be tortured – and both Palla and Huttser fought with ferocity, driven by love and desire to spare their mate the suffering of long death. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />Unfortunately, I can’t say that the rest of Palla and Huttser’s pack were developed with the same care – mostly because they don’t survive for long. We had Khaz and Kipcha as a strong beta couple, old nursemaid Brassa harbouring her own secrets, and cowardly omega Bran who turned out to be braver than everyone thought him to be. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[All of them had potential, but they ultimately were killed off before they could play any major role in the story. I understand the necessity of their fate being dictated by the plot, but that makes me feel as if these characters were wasted. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>The plot</b> of the story is truly captivating, though I have certain issues with the pacing. Even though it escalates quickly after the beginning, and there are plenty of deaths so no character is truly safe, there are also long and drawn-out chapters devoted to building up Larka’s relationships or her training with the Sight that honestly felt like a bore. However, once the action starts in the earnest and pace picks up, the story absorbs you completely. <b>The mystical elements</b> of it were certainly the best part, and I find that the prophecies and legends interwoven into the plot lend it a delightfully eerie atmosphere. Spiritual world blends with the world of living through the powers of the Sight, and seeing them manifest is always awe-inspiring. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[With Larka exploring her abilities, we learn more and more about the Sight as she travels on the wings of birds and sees future and past in the water – but when Morgra uses the Sight and calls on the Searchers, we actually witness for the first time what I thought would be impossible even with the power of the Sight, calling spectres of the dead to fight the living. The massacre at Kosov, while ruthless, was a turning point in the story, and the fact that Larka had to go into the lands of dead to call them off was truly thrilling. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> And yet even though supernatural is an integral part of the wolves’ world, sometimes it’s questioned and even challenged, and I appreciate that David Clement-Davies allows a variety of interpretations when it comes to the mystic elements. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[For example, I still don’t have a clear answer as to what exactly happened with Fell when he was supposedly possessed by Wolfbane. Both Larka and Morgra say that Wolfbane is just a name in a story, so it's most likely that Fell was brainwashed by Morgra, fed on her lies and hate and pain, and all of that combined with trauma till he couldn't remember anything of his old life... But then, Tsarr did see a ghostly grey wolf near Fell - so does it actually mean that Fell was truly possessed? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />For me the story really shined not only due to the plot, but also because of <b>the villains</b> Larka and her family had to face. <b>Morgra</b> was especially fascinating to me as a character. She’s not entirely unsympathetic, as she was denied her place in the pack and cast out for something that was only partly her fault, but eventually, it was Morgra’s choice to use her Sight for her own gain and to become feared once she realized that she’s not going to be loved. Morgra is using superstition and fear of myths to manipulate the Balkar and scare them into obedience, but it’s unclear whether she believes in the legends herself or not. She dismisses the myth of Wolfbane, yet continues to use his name when it benefits her, and I rather like this development. The same prophecy that dictates Larka’s fate also determines Morgra’s destiny, but she twists its words and finds a way around it. Sometimes Morgra may seem to be over-the-top cliche villain who is destined to be evil, but her PoV shows her to be a truly manipulative and crafty mastermind, and that's why I love reading her chapters.<br /><br />But what I find especially interesting is that even those who oppose Morgra are not automatically portrayed as heroic and righteous. <b>Slavka</b> is a strong-willed she-wolf leading the rebellion against Morgra who managed to unite the free wolves into the Greater Pack. Her goal to fight Morgra and create a better future for the others is just, but in doing so she employs methods that are beyond harsh. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Slavka executes strict ‘if you’re not with us, you are against us’ policy as she forces wolves to join the Greater Pack against their will, forbids religion and all spiritual practices and executes any who dares oppose her with cruelty that can make Morgra jealous – just ask Palla and Huttser. I’m not sure if Slavka wished it or not, but she cultivated such a strong cult of the leader in her pack that she established order that’s almost as bad as Morgra’s. At least Slavka was able to realize her wrongs in the end and amend them, and that’s the difference between her and Morgra. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Tsarr and Skart</b> are also among those who fight against Morgra, though the means they chose to do so are vastly different from Slavka’s: while Slavka relies on planning and strength in numbers, denying anything supernatural, Tsarr also possesses the Sight, and so he and his companion Skart take it upon themselves to educate Larka about her gift and direct her on the path destined for her. The characters of Tsarr and Skart left me conflicted, because even though they were never portrayed in negative light like Slavka, I didn’t exactly trust them. It’s true that they didn’t have any interior motives regarding Larka, but they were so focused on fulfilling the prophecy that they ignored anything that they viewed as negligible. At one point, it’s revealed that Skart had been watching after Larka’s pack for a long time, but never showed himself or interfered, justifying it by the fact that Larka wasn’t ready to be taught yet. However, just a little help and a warning could have saved Bran and prevented Kar and Fell from being separated from their family, sparing the whole pack a lot of pain and suffering. And the fact that Skart chose not to act when the calamities befalling Larka’s family weren’t affecting the prophecy makes it very difficult for me to actually like him or Tsarr.<br /><br />Sadly, <b>wolf mythology</b> in ‘The Sight’ turned out to be a real disappointment for me. David Clement-Davies had obviously worked hard on the world-building, coming up with the terminology the wolves use and the laws and customs by which the packs operate, and I have no complaints about it. However, I have complaints about the way it was delivered to the readers – namely, info dumps. I actually love getting information about the world’s history and lore, so normally I don't mind info dumps and don't get it when people complain about them; however, in ‘The Sight’ they were done really clumsily, either as random facts put into the middle of unrelated scenes or as the characters asking each other things they are supposed to know already. And the mythology itself didn’t impress me, either, as it drew heavily on Bible and Christian scripture. Usually, I don’t mind some allegories to existing religions, as similar themes can be found in many different mythologies and folklores all over the globe. However, in ‘The Sight’ we get legends and fables that copy the Bible to a detail, except being modified to fit into the wolves’ world, including the fall of Lucifer, the curse of Cain and Jesus's sacrifice. I like books that make me think, but in this case I felt that David Clement-Davies either dived too deep into philosophy that had nothing to do with the story or that he didn’t have enough imagination to create his own lore.<br /><br />There were <b>other issues</b> that stopped me from fully enjoying the story, though they were not deal-breakers. For example, I was annoyed that the ravens were portrayed negatively, being associated with evil Wolfbane and well as serving as Morgra’s spies – and while it’s interesting to see the trend of wolf packs being allies with ravens broken in this manner, I’m still slightly peeved that 'scavengers' are portrayed as wicked again. But what made me more than a little angry is the way ‘The Sight’ painted humans as inheritably evil. At first, I assumed that the very perspective of wolves who view all humans as cruel and evil was subjective, and that the conflict between them lies in the simple fact that while wolves think it their right to take down whatever prey they can, humans also have valid reasons to hunt down predators who steal from their herds… <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[except that later in Larka’s vision it’s the whole human civilization and the constant advance of its cities on nature that are revealed to be the ultimate threat that’s going to destroy the wild world. Maybe the message on environmental problems is an important one, but I don’t enjoy being beaten over the head with it. In this context, Larka ‘adopting’ the human child, Bran, was a concept that had a lot of potential, but eventually, Bran simply tagged along with the wolves, playing a role in fulfilling the prophecy but not changing the way wolves actually see humans. It also irked me how the narration kept constantly switching between referring to the human child as 'it' and 'him', sometimes even in the span of two paragraphs. At first I sought a hidden meaning to this, thinking that maybe such a technique is used to reflect the views of different characters, like if Morgra and Slavka referred to Bran as 'it' while Larka used 'him', but the narration did it completely randomly! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Overall, while ‘The Sight’ had a cast of three-dimensional characters and expertly handled some difficult moral themes, too many things dampened my enjoyment of the story, resulting in it receiving only 3 stars – which is quite a low rating by my standards.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10724731138226720079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer2646168767025737356">This book was recommended and learn to me by a friend and it took me a long time to pick it up. One of those books that I started and just could not get past the first chapter for whatever reason. Determined to be able to return my friend’s books I preserved and in hindsight I am glad. What a poignant story! <br/><br/>It’s so many things in one - superstitions, anthropomorphic, animal instincts, human instincts, lore and tradition, evil and good, truth and lies, hope, family. <br/><br/>It was truly very interesting</span>
  <span id="freeText2646168767025737356" style="display:none">This book was recommended and learn to me by a friend and it took me a long time to pick it up. One of those books that I started and just could not get past the first chapter for whatever reason. Determined to be able to return my friend’s books I preserved and in hindsight I am glad. What a poignant story! <br /><br />It’s so many things in one - superstitions, anthropomorphic, animal instincts, human instincts, lore and tradition, evil and good, truth and lies, hope, family. <br /><br />It was truly very interesting and it’s obviously a lot of research went into this book - from the wolves and their instincts and packs and actions to tying in Transylvanian history to the moral of this story. <br /><br />I will say this is NOT where I saw the book going. I did guess who Wolfbane was but I didn’t anticipate Larka’s death, the family reuniting, or the warning against war and pollution. </span>

  <span id="freeText8833932297350235753" style="display:none">I got this book as gift from a friend. I read half of it about 6 years ago and just picked it back up. <br /><br />I was not a fan of the authors style and felt the book could have easily been condensed to half its length while not compromising the story. <br /><br />The undertones of environmentalism and anti religion attitudes I found annoying as well as the assumption that people are bad and just destroy the world. Ultimately I found the book to be nihilistic and the story just not strong enough to counter balance it. <br /><br />The research on wolf behaviour was also quit weak though I recognize it is fantasy. <br /><br />Beautiful cover and some nice moments in the book. My one star report is my personal analyses of the content but if this book is your style it could be a fun read. </span>

<div id="review_2328660838" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2328660838" />
    <a title="Jennifer Wheeler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16578356-jennifer-wheeler"><img alt="Jennifer Wheeler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588377594p2/16578356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2328660838?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Wheeler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Wheeler" href="/user/show/16578356-jennifer-wheeler">Jennifer Wheeler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780142408742" href="/book/show/1634915.The_Sight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16578356-jennifer-wheeler?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16578356-jennifer-wheeler?shelf=moms-books">moms-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16578356-jennifer-wheeler?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2328660838&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2328660838" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6677802993128574686">Not terrible, but not amazing either. I liked the author’s style of descriptive writing, but the heavy Christian-esque element to the storyline put me off at first. Larka’s vision, and ultimately the ending, definitely wasn’t something that I foresaw coming.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_108876305" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/108876305" />
    <a title="Crystal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3926585-crystal"><img alt="Crystal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370264585p2/3926585.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/108876305?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Crystal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Crystal" href="/user/show/3926585-crystal">Crystal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926585-crystal?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926585-crystal?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926585-crystal?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926585-crystal?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926585-crystal?shelf=epic-fantasy">epic-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer108876305" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5455953971656380605"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/32665.David_Clement_Davies' title='David Clement-Davies' rel='nofollow noopener'>David Clement-Davies</a> creates a unique culture and belief system for his characters by blending religious paradigms from various religions. Using this, he creates the basis for a world in which rich, engaging characters come to life from the pages. Although the plot is not necessarily as inventive as this environment he has created, it is a wonderful tale about fate & destiny, friendship, family, and growing into one's self against all odds.<br/><br/>From the moment you meet the pack, the reader feels a un</span>
  <span id="freeText5455953971656380605" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/32665.David_Clement_Davies" title="David Clement-Davies" rel="nofollow noopener">David Clement-Davies</a> creates a unique culture and belief system for his characters by blending religious paradigms from various religions. Using this, he creates the basis for a world in which rich, engaging characters come to life from the pages. Although the plot is not necessarily as inventive as this environment he has created, it is a wonderful tale about fate &amp; destiny, friendship, family, and growing into one's self against all odds.<br /><br />From the moment you meet the pack, the reader feels a unique bond to each wolf. They all have their own strengths and shortcomings with personalities that stand out. Their beliefs and culture, as mentioned earlier, are based on many paradigms that exist in our real world blended together flawlessly. In many books, whenever tales are told about heroes or deities, it can get a bit daunting and trying because it's often so irrelevant. But <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/32665.David_Clement_Davies" title="David Clement-Davies" rel="nofollow noopener">David Clement-Davies</a> manages to avoid that and instead gives the reader greater insight with relevant tales. What is also perhaps the most surprising is that the antagonist is not entirely deplorable. She is written in a way that it's hard not to feel some sympathy for her alongside the contempt.<br /><br />And although the plot is generally depressing, there is a lot of loss and heartache throughout, in the end it is an uplifting story. For the characters to persevere through what they had, it takes a great deal of strength, courage, and strong ties to one another. And at its core, although the book avoided being didactic, I think that was part of the intended takeaway message from the book: to rely not just on your own strength but the support and strength of those who love you to carry you through the storms.<br /><br />I think perhaps the only pitfall of this book is somewhat in the writing style. It's not necessarily the best style I've ever read nor is it by any stretch of the imagination perfect, but it did grate on the nerves a little as the other abused the same words over and over again for when the animals spoke amongst one another. On the other hand, however, I can say it's certainly better than the alternative where author's try to avoid using the word "said" by using every and any other word to describe verbal communication.<br /><br />Overall, this book is an excellent coming of age story in a fantasy setting. It's technically a young adult book, but I believe that people of all ages can enjoy the story as it unfolds as well as appreciate and enjoy the characters with their relationships to one another.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5455953971656380605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_164090981" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/164090981" />
    <a title="Sinn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4693342-sinn"><img alt="Sinn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501738741p2/4693342.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/164090981?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sinn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sinn" href="/user/show/4693342-sinn">Sinn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164090981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4741732401624938498">I picked up this book because I love wolves. It caught my attention several times, but I never bothered to buy it. However, when I found it at the library, I jumped at the opportunity! Even though I had an idea it dealt with wolves, I was not ready for a beast fable. It reminds me of Watership Down.<br/><br/>The storytelling in this book was intricate and simply beautiful. Clement-Davies has a way with words and is able to craft a beautiful world that is very three-dimensional and easy to get into. Even t</span>
  <span id="freeText4741732401624938498" style="display:none">I picked up this book because I love wolves. It caught my attention several times, but I never bothered to buy it. However, when I found it at the library, I jumped at the opportunity! Even though I had an idea it dealt with wolves, I was not ready for a beast fable. It reminds me of Watership Down.<br /><br />The storytelling in this book was intricate and simply beautiful. Clement-Davies has a way with words and is able to craft a beautiful world that is very three-dimensional and easy to get into. Even though it follows wolves as the central characters, I felt a kinship and closeness with them that is sometimes is hard to find in books following humans. He pulls Christian myth/themes very heavily into the book. But, even with that, it is a very, very dark story. It permeates the whole book and sets the mood. To me, it was effective.<br /><br />That being said, I found this book rather hard to get into. At several points, I would force myself to read "just one more page." I found it interesting that the library shelves this book with adult sci-fi while it is actually geared toward the younger reader (12+). At that age, I would have had a hard time not putting this book down. It is extremely dense. Usually it takes me a week or less to read a book, and I found myself struggling through for 2 weeks! I enjoy reads that make you think and engage the reader, but this book just made me feel bogged down. While the majority of the book is a build up to the final conflict, I wondered if he could have done it in a less wordy/dense way. It wasn't until the last 20 pages that I felt the pace actually picked up. Yes, the final conflict will invariably bring action, there needs to be a little more interest in a book.<br /><br />Aside from my feelings of being bogged down, my biggest issues is the author's lack of research into real wolves. The second breeding pair in the pack is an absolutely no-no. Also, the nearness of rival packs without the main pack knowing is unbelievable. And a whole pack will rear pups. It could have taken him a few minutes to skim a book and get this easily accessed information. Even though the story is beautifully crafted, this actually undermines it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4741732401624938498" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_172673451" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172673451" />
    <a title="Amber 7-3" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3066603-amber-7-3"><img alt="Amber 7-3" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172673451?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amber 7-3" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amber 7-3" href="/user/show/3066603-amber-7-3">Amber 7-3</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172673451" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15861601738119353772">The moment I stepped in that tiny bookshop, I knew something powerful was headed my way. I held a slow pace, taking my time, not missing a single book on all the rows. I let my hand rise up, sliding over the coarse spines. I kept this up for a while. Then all of a sudden, something caught my attention. My eyes fluttered across a small, white book, with piercing yellow staring back at me. I picked it up, knowing that that petite peice of literature would take me to a place of wonder...and so I fl</span>
  <span id="freeText15861601738119353772" style="display:none">The moment I stepped in that tiny bookshop, I knew something powerful was headed my way. I held a slow pace, taking my time, not missing a single book on all the rows. I let my hand rise up, sliding over the coarse spines. I kept this up for a while. Then all of a sudden, something caught my attention. My eyes fluttered across a small, white book, with piercing yellow staring back at me. I picked it up, knowing that that petite peice of literature would take me to a place of wonder...and so I flipped to the first page, and started to read.<br /><br />I truly believe "The Sight" to be one of the greates books I have ever read, possibly the best. David Clement-Davies weaves a captivating tale through his influential words. They role off your tounge like a nice bowl of icecream. He beautifully displays the life of a young wolf destined for greatness...and doom. Her name is Larka, and she was born with sight, the ability to see into the minds and hearts of other creatures. Further, it allows her to see images, of both past, present, and future, through water. From beginning to end her story take her through loss, pain, growth, love, and ultimite sacrifice. This book has opened my mind, and let me experience the world in a whole new view. Battling the shadows of her past, and the possible flower of her future, Larka significantly reminds me of myself; my own life. I have learned much about life from this book. I have learned that love is the greatest of all virtues, and sometimes we must let go of things, to ensure the safety of the ones we hold dearest. Most of all "The Sight" has taught me about myself; has helped me decide what I charish, and what I'm willing to give up. I have learned about new passions of mine, and watched what I once thought important, grow insignificant. This entrancing book has taken me through a journey, a transformation. I love everything about this writing. I love the detailed characters, the beautiful scenery, and the twisting plot. I never once stopped to put this book down, and I intend to watch time float by reading it again and again. "The Sight" is truly a five star book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15861601738119353772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_409016853" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/409016853" />
    <a title="InkCreature" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12356522-inkcreature"><img alt="InkCreature" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439870751p2/12356522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/409016853?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="InkCreature" class="user" itemprop="url" name="InkCreature" href="/user/show/12356522-inkcreature">InkCreature</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer409016853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16772657347319636989">This is my all time favourite book ever written, this and Watership Down.<br/><br/>It has the most descriptive, beautiful writing ever mastered, I have never come across any other author that could write more skillfully than David Clement-Davies. This is why he is my all time favourite author. Though I find it disturbing that this book was in the children's section considering how dark, disturbing and gory most of it's content is. It is clearly meant for older audiences. I read this when I was around 12 a</span>
  <span id="freeText16772657347319636989" style="display:none">This is my all time favourite book ever written, this and Watership Down.<br /><br />It has the most descriptive, beautiful writing ever mastered, I have never come across any other author that could write more skillfully than David Clement-Davies. This is why he is my all time favourite author. Though I find it disturbing that this book was in the children's section considering how dark, disturbing and gory most of it's content is. It is clearly meant for older audiences. I read this when I was around 12 and I still love it as my favourite book. I can't find books even half as good as this nowadays. The storyline is packed with adventure and mystery. The author does an amazing job at letting the reader know he's the boss and he can kill a loved character whenever he so wishes. This is reminds the reader that they aren't in control which is better I think because then the story is more unpredictable and keeps the reader more on their toes. This is how all writers should write.<br /><br />Though a morbid story it feels satisfyingly finished at the end, because there is purpose and meaning in everything that happens in the book and the book would not feel so fulfilling if morbid things like that did not happen. It's also full of hope and love, showing just how deep loves go and written in the best way possible with the best examples.<br /><br />This book lines up a lot with the Bible as well as Biblical values and it is just...so amazing. Though it's all used in metaphors, nothing could ever be more magically written than this. <br /><br />Although the ending is really REALLY sad, I feel that it was actually a perfect ending to the story because it's fulfilling and full of purpose. Fell ruins the first book just by claiming that the ending was a lie and therefore I hate Fell just for that. I wish Fell had never existed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16772657347319636989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1331639298" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1331639298" />
    <a title="Shelby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3737963-shelby"><img alt="Shelby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274026137p2/3737963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1331639298?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shelby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shelby" href="/user/show/3737963-shelby">Shelby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1331639298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13462703154103687494">Ugh.<br/><br/>I'm disappointed in myself. I used to love this book. I bought it back in middle school because I really liked wolves and I really liked fantasy and I really liked big books. There is no better definition of a recipe for success.<br/><br/>This review is kind of going to have two parts, because I am of two minds about this book. For instance, when I was in middle school/high school...<br/><br/>I freaking LOVED this book. Why?<br/><br/>Because wolves.<br/><br/>Wolves who were like little people and had little families and went on qu</span>
  <span id="freeText13462703154103687494" style="display:none">Ugh.<br /><br />I'm disappointed in myself. I used to love this book. I bought it back in middle school because I really liked wolves and I really liked fantasy and I really liked big books. There is no better definition of a recipe for success.<br /><br />This review is kind of going to have two parts, because I am of two minds about this book. For instance, when I was in middle school/high school...<br /><br />I freaking LOVED this book. Why?<br /><br />Because wolves.<br /><br />Wolves who were like little people and had little families and went on quests and saved the world.<br /><br />Special wolves. In a very long book that I could spend lots of time reading with pretty prose and also wolves.<br /><br />If we shoot forward to now, 23 year old me kind of hates this book.<br /><br />I mean, I don't really hate it. It's still the same book I used to love. It just doesn't hold that same magic anymore. Maybe it's because I quite simply read it too much. I knew the story so well it was more like trudging through an extremely long mud field to get to the end. And I didn't enjoy it as much.<br /><br />That's okay though, because we're supposed to grow out of things. We age and mature (and get really awesome, in my case), and the things we like sometimes change too.<br /><br />So, here's the deal. This book is about wolves, but they talk to each other and have a religion and all sorts of other things. I have both loved and not loved this book at varying times, so if it sounds intriguing to you I definitely think you should pick it up. Or give it to a middle schooler you know who likes wild animals.<br /><br />Incidentally, I actually think Fire Bringer is a more well written book than this one. So there's that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13462703154103687494" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_259200479" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/259200479" />
    <a title="Emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7229656-emily"><img alt="Emily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325875746p2/7229656.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/259200479?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/7229656-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer259200479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7853191142696262139">Probably my favorite book of all time. No joke here, folks. <br/><br/>I understand that it was designed for younger readers, around the early teen areas, and I admit that is how old I was when I first read this book. But, I have read it again and continue to love it just as deeply as I did as a junior high student. <br/><br/>The character development? The setting? The plot? All stupendous. <br/><br/>I loved this book so much I made an attempt to make contact with David Clement-Davies himself to ask him questions about the hi</span>
  <span id="freeText7853191142696262139" style="display:none">Probably my favorite book of all time. No joke here, folks. <br /><br />I understand that it was designed for younger readers, around the early teen areas, and I admit that is how old I was when I first read this book. But, I have read it again and continue to love it just as deeply as I did as a junior high student. <br /><br />The character development? The setting? The plot? All stupendous. <br /><br />I loved this book so much I made an attempt to make contact with David Clement-Davies himself to ask him questions about the history in the book, and a hope to gain the knowledge of a variety of unrelated unnecessary facts regarding this book.<br /><br />In short: I was pretty obsessed. <br /><br />Reading it again at an older age made me understand the characters a little bit more, understand the complexity of the story, and the motivations behind some characters that I might not have understood before. <br /><br />Speaking of characters, by giving the wolves so much humanity, there were moments when I forgot that they were not actually human (easy mistake). <br /><br />After reading this book a total of six times (I believe) the characters and adventure does not get old. Despite age, The Sight will be enjoyed.<br /><br />For older readers, the wording will be incredibly easy to understand, but the depth of the characters and the excitement of the plot and adventures are ageless.<br /><br />I hope many find the time to pick this book up, because I do believe that it has affected my life, especially my younger teens.<br /><br />Enjoy and read happy, my friends. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="7853191142696262139" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rB50MHcG/l7rQU434qiWbaPZjr03hpJiljUhNsGPoqVQBIXu/GFsnB79VA61x32GlDWz/HKmmDNI8wK9l76REw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wDikdysdgzU+QXCT+RSO55q6h5MKzSg0Rb2/rMH4NQo8IlWpoHoR98v9aqque2UMrVa60k/tImWbe5wnl8kGvA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

But Shade and his Brightwing friend Marina, now young adults, discover that the humans have a sinister motive for befriending the bats--they are using them as unwitting suicide bombers over a jungle war zone. This time he has a plan for making his god, Zotz, supreme: to be explicit (and Oppel is), by ripping out the hearts of 100 imprisoned bats, owls, and rats. Shade\'s and Marina\'s race to save their companions from this two-pronged threat makes for exciting and occasionally terrifying reading.        Once again Oppel immerses readers in the world view of his tiny flying mammals. It becomes second nature to see things upside down, hide in crevices, squint at the brightness of the sun, and sense danger through sound vibrations. Particularly chilling is his portrayal of the humans\' laboratory, with its concentration-camp-like indifference to life. In Sunwing, Oppel offers breathless suspense while eliciting our compassion for these misunderstood creatures of the night. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3209081408139379929">David was born in 1964 and went to Westminster School and Edinburgh University. There, Clement-Davies read History and English Literature, specializing in the Italian Renaissance, and Russian Literature and Society. For many years, he dreamed of one day becoming an actor taking a drama course and working in theater. However, he was also interested in writing and soon became a freelance travel jour</span>
  <span id="freeText3209081408139379929" style="display:none">David was born in 1964 and went to Westminster School and Edinburgh University. There, Clement-Davies read History and English Literature, specializing in the Italian Renaissance, and Russian Literature and Society. For many years, he dreamed of one day becoming an actor taking a drama course and working in theater. However, he was also interested in writing and soon became a freelance travel journalist.<br /><br />Clement-Davies lived in a little mountain home in Andalusia region of Spain to write The Sight, has traveled the world and now also lives in London. He has also written a musical, two adult novels, and a play, set in the present and the 17th century, called Startled Anatomies, alongside his children’s books. His online publishing website is phoeniarkpress.com and from there he is trying to create a grass roots publisher.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3209081408139379929" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
